Earlier works of ADMM include [27, 26, 25, 20], and recently it has gained increasing popularity, in part due to [11]. We refer to Appendix C for the expression of ℱDRsubscriptℱDR\mathcal{F}_{{}_{\mathrm{DR}}} and more discussions between the equivalence between ADMM and Douglas–Rachford splitting.Based on such an equivalence, the convergence property of ADMM has been well studied in the literature, we refer to [30, 21, 18, 31] and the references therein.In [5], uk,ψksubscript𝑢𝑘subscript𝜓𝑘u_{k},\psi_{k} are called the “shadow sequences” of Douglas–Rachford splitting, in this paper, following the terminology, we shall call xk,yksubscript𝑥𝑘subscript𝑦𝑘x_{k},y_{k} of (1.1) the shadow sequences of ADMM.

The contribution of our paper is threefold. First,for the sequence {zk}k∈ℕsubscriptsubscript𝑧𝑘𝑘ℕ\{{z}_{k}\}_{k\in\mathbb{N}} of (1.2), we show that it has two different types of trajectory:•When both R,J𝑅𝐽R,J are non-smooth functions, under the assumption that they are partly smooth (see Definition 2.1), we show that the eventual trajectory of {zk}k∈ℕsubscriptsubscript𝑧𝑘𝑘ℕ\{{z}_{k}\}_{k\in\mathbb{N}} is approximately a spiral which can be characterized precisely if R,J𝑅𝐽R,J are moreover locally polyhedral around the solution.•When at least one of R,J𝑅𝐽R,J is smooth, we show that depends on the choice of γ𝛾\gamma, the eventual trajectory of {zk}k∈ℕsubscriptsubscript𝑧𝑘𝑘ℕ\{{z}_{k}\}_{k\in\mathbb{N}} can be either straight line or spiral.Second, based on trajectory of {zk}k∈ℕsubscriptsubscript𝑧𝑘𝑘ℕ\{{z}_{k}\}_{k\in\mathbb{N}}, we discuss the limitations of the current combination of ADMM and inertial acceleration technique. In Section 3, we distinguish the situations where inertial acceleration will work and when it fails. More precisely, we find that inertial technique will work if the trajectory of {zk}k∈ℕsubscriptsubscript𝑧𝑘𝑘ℕ\{{z}_{k}\}_{k\in\mathbb{N}} is or close to a straight line, and will fail if the trajectory is a spiral.

Our core contribution is an adaptive acceleration for ADMM, which is inspired by the trajectory of ADMM and dubbed “A33\!{}^{3}DMM”. The limitation of inertial technique, particularly its failure, implies that the right acceleration scheme should be able to follow the trajectory of the iterates. In Section 4, we propose an adaptive extrapolation scheme for accelerating ADMM which is able to following the trajectory of the generated sequence.Our proposed A33\!{}^{3}DMM belongs to the realm of extrapolation method, and provides an alternative geometrical interpretation for polynomial extrapolation methods such as Minimal Polynomial Extrapolation (MPE) [14] and Reduced Rank Extrapolation (RRE) [22, 39].

Over the past decades, owing to the tremendous success of inertial acceleration [41, 9], the inertial technique has been widely adapted to accelerate other first-order algorithms. In terms of ADMM, related work can be found in [43, 32, 24], either from proximal point algorithm perspective or continuous dynamical system. However, to ensure that inertial acceleration works, stronger assumptions are imposed on R,J𝑅𝐽R,J in (𝒫ADMMsubscript𝒫ADMM\mathcal{P}_{\mathrm{ADMM}}), such as smooth differentiability or strong convexity. When it comes to general non-smooth problems, these works may fail to provide acceleration. Recently in [23], an O​(1/k2)𝑂1superscript𝑘2O(1/k^{2}) convergence rate is established for ADMM using Nesterov acceleration, however the result holds only for the continuous dynamical system while the discrete-time optimization scheme remains unavailable.

For more generic acceleration techniques, there are extensive works in numerical analysis on the topic of convergence acceleration for sequences. The goal of convergence acceleration is, given an arbitrary sequence {zk}k∈ℕ⊂ℝnsubscriptsubscript𝑧𝑘𝑘ℕsuperscriptℝ𝑛\{{z}_{k}\}_{k\in\mathbb{N}}\subset\mathbb{R}^{n} with limit z⋆superscript𝑧⋆{z}^{\star}, finding a transformation ℰk:{zk−j}j=1q→z¯k∈ℝn:subscriptℰ𝑘→superscriptsubscriptsubscript𝑧𝑘𝑗𝑗1𝑞subscript¯𝑧𝑘superscriptℝ𝑛\mathcal{E}_{k}:\{z_{k-j}\}_{j=1}^{q}\to{\bar{z}}_{k}\in\mathbb{R}^{n} such that z¯ksubscript¯𝑧𝑘{\bar{z}}_{k} converges faster to z⋆superscript𝑧⋆{z}^{\star}. In general, the process by which {zk}subscript𝑧𝑘\{{z}_{k}\} is generated is unknown, q𝑞q is chosen to be a small integer, and z¯ksubscript¯𝑧𝑘{\bar{z}}_{k} is referred to as the extrapolation of zksubscript𝑧𝑘{z}_{k}. Some of the best known examples include Richardson’s extrapolation [45], the Δ2superscriptΔ2\Delta^{2}-process of Aitken [2] and Shank’s algorithm [47].We refer to [12, 13, 48] and references therein for a detailed historical perspective on the development of these techniques.Much of the works on the extrapolation of vector sequences was initiated by Wynn [53] who generalized the work of Shank to vector sequences.In the appendix, the formulation of some of these methods are provided. In particular, minimal polynomial extrapolation (MPE) [14] and Reduced Rank Extrapolation (RRE) [22, 39] (which is also a variant of Anderson acceleration developed independently in [4]), which are particularly relevant to this present work (see Section 4.2 for brief discussion).

More recently, there has been a series of work on a regularized version of RRE stemming from [46]. We remark however that the regularisation parameter in these works rely on a grid search based on objective function, their applicability to the general ADMM setting is unclear.

Denote ℝnsuperscriptℝ𝑛\mathbb{R}^{n} a n𝑛n-dimensional Euclidean space equipped with scalar inner product ⟨⋅,⋅⟩⋅⋅\langle\cdot,\,\cdot\rangle and norm ||⋅||{|\kern-1.125pt|}\cdot{|\kern-1.125pt|}. IdId\mathrm{Id} denotes the identity operator on ℝnsuperscriptℝ𝑛\mathbb{R}^{n}. Γ0​(ℝn)subscriptΓ0superscriptℝ𝑛\Gamma_{0}(\mathbb{R}^{n}) denotes the class of proper convex and lower-semicontinuous functions on ℝnsuperscriptℝ𝑛\mathbb{R}^{n}.For a nonempty convex set S⊂ℝn𝑆superscriptℝ𝑛S\subset\mathbb{R}^{n}, denote ri​(S)ri𝑆\mathrm{ri}(S) its relative interior, par​(S)par𝑆\mathrm{par}(S) the smallest subspace parallel to S𝑆S and 𝒫Ssubscript𝒫𝑆\mathscr{P}_{S} the projection operator onto S𝑆S.The sub-differential of a function R∈Γ0​(ℝn)𝑅subscriptΓ0superscriptℝ𝑛R\in\Gamma_{0}(\mathbb{R}^{n}) is a set-valued mapping defined by ∂R​(x)=def{g∈ℝn|R​(x′)≥R​(x)+⟨g,x′−x⟩,∀x′∈ℝn}superscriptdef𝑅𝑥conditional-set𝑔superscriptℝ𝑛formulae-sequence𝑅superscript𝑥′𝑅𝑥𝑔superscript𝑥′𝑥for-allsuperscript𝑥′superscriptℝ𝑛\partial R(x)\stackrel{{\scriptstyle\text{\rm\tiny def}}}{{=}}\big{\{}g\in\mathbb{R}^{n}|R(x^{\prime})\geq R(x)+\langle g,\,x^{\prime}-x\rangle,\forall x^{\prime}\in\mathbb{R}^{n}\big{\}}.The spectral radius of a matrix M𝑀M is denoted by ρ​(M)𝜌𝑀\rho(M).

The rest of paper is organized as following: in Section 2 we study the trajectory of ADMM for the sequence {zk}k∈ℕsubscriptsubscript𝑧𝑘𝑘ℕ\{{z}_{k}\}_{k\in\mathbb{N}}. The limitation of the inertial technique is discussed in Section 3. Then in Section 4, we propose the adaptive acceleration scheme for ADMM followed by detailed discussions provided in Section 5. Numerical experiments from machine learning and imaging are provided in Section 6. In the appendix, we first provide extra discussions on why inertial fails, and then the proofs of main propositions.

In this section, we discuss the trajectory of the sequence {zk}k∈ℕsubscriptsubscript𝑧𝑘𝑘ℕ\{{z}_{k}\}_{k\in\mathbb{N}} generated by ADMM based on the concept “partial smoothness” which was first introduced in [34].

The difficulty of analyzing the trajectory, or in general the behaviors (e.g. convergence rate), of ADMM is that the iteration of the method is non-linear. We need a proper tool of (locally) linearize the iteration of ADMM, and partial smoothness provides a powerful framework to achieve the goal.Let ℳ⊂ℝnℳsuperscriptℝ𝑛\mathcal{M}\subset\mathbb{R}^{n} be a C2superscript𝐶2C^{2}-smooth Riemannian manifold, denote 𝒯ℳ​(x)subscript𝒯ℳ𝑥\mathcal{T}_{\mathcal{M}}(x) the tangent space of ℳℳ\mathcal{M} at a point x∈ℳ𝑥ℳx\in\mathcal{M}.

The class of partly smooth functions at x¯¯𝑥\bar{x} relative to ℳx¯subscriptℳ¯𝑥\mathcal{M}_{\bar{x}} is denoted as PSFx¯​(ℳx¯)subscriptPSF¯𝑥subscriptℳ¯𝑥\mathrm{PSF}_{\bar{x}}(\mathcal{M}_{\bar{x}}).Popular examples of partly smooth functions can be found in [35, Chapter 5].Loosely speaking, a partly smooth function behaves smoothly along ℳx¯subscriptℳ¯𝑥\mathcal{M}_{\bar{x}}, and sharply normal to it. The essence of partial smoothness is that the behaviour of the function and of its minimizers depend essentially on its restriction to this manifold, hence providing the possibilities to study the trajectory of sequences.

Next we discuss the trajectory of ADMM in terms of {zk}k∈ℕsubscriptsubscript𝑧𝑘𝑘ℕ\{{z}_{k}\}_{k\in\mathbb{N}}. The iteration of ADMM is non-linear in general owing to the non-linearity of the proximity mappings. However, when R,J𝑅𝐽R,J are partly smooth, the local C2superscript𝐶2C^{2}-smoothness allows us to linearize the proximity mappings, hence the ADMM iteration. In turn, this allows us to study the trajectory of sequence generated by the method.We denote (x⋆,y⋆,ψ⋆)superscript𝑥⋆superscript𝑦⋆superscript𝜓⋆(x^{\star},y^{\star},\psi^{\star}) a saddle-point of ℒ​(x,y;ψ)ℒ𝑥𝑦𝜓\mathcal{L}(x,y;\psi) and let z⋆=ψ⋆+γ​A​x⋆superscript𝑧⋆superscript𝜓⋆𝛾𝐴superscript𝑥⋆{z}^{\star}=\psi^{\star}+\gamma Ax^{\star}.

Denote vk=defzk−zk−1superscriptdefsubscript𝑣𝑘subscript𝑧𝑘subscript𝑧𝑘1v_{k}\stackrel{{\scriptstyle\text{\rm\tiny def}}}{{=}}{z}_{k}-{z}_{k-1} and let θk=defarccos⁡(⟨vk,vk−1⟩‖vk‖​‖vk−1‖)superscriptdefsubscript𝜃𝑘subscript𝑣𝑘subscript𝑣𝑘1normsubscript𝑣𝑘normsubscript𝑣𝑘1\theta_{k}\stackrel{{\scriptstyle\text{\rm\tiny def}}}{{=}}\arccos\big{(}{\frac{\langle v_{k},\,v_{k-1}\rangle}{{|\kern-1.125pt|}v_{k}{|\kern-1.125pt|}{|\kern-1.125pt|}v_{k-1}{|\kern-1.125pt|}}}\big{)} be the angle between vk,vk−1subscript𝑣𝑘subscript𝑣𝑘1v_{k},v_{k-1}. We shall use {θk}k∈ℕsubscriptsubscript𝜃𝑘𝑘ℕ\{\theta_{k}\}_{k\in\mathbb{N}} to characterize the trajectory of {zk}k∈ℕsubscriptsubscript𝑧𝑘𝑘ℕ\{{z}_{k}\}_{k\in\mathbb{N}}. Given a saddle point (x⋆,y⋆,ψ⋆)superscript𝑥⋆superscript𝑦⋆superscript𝜓⋆(x^{\star},y^{\star},\psi^{\star}), the corresponding KKT condition entails −AT​ψ⋆∈∂R​(x⋆)superscript𝐴𝑇superscript𝜓⋆𝑅superscript𝑥⋆-A^{T}\psi^{\star}\in{\partial R(x^{\star})} and −BT​ψ⋆∈∂J​(y⋆)superscript𝐵𝑇superscript𝜓⋆𝐽superscript𝑦⋆-B^{T}\psi^{\star}\in{\partial J(y^{\star})}, below we impose that−AT​ψ⋆∈ri​(∂R​(x⋆))and−BT​ψ⋆∈ri​(∂J​(y⋆)).formulae-sequencesuperscript𝐴𝑇superscript𝜓⋆ri𝑅superscript𝑥⋆andsuperscript𝐵𝑇superscript𝜓⋆ri𝐽superscript𝑦⋆-A^{T}\psi^{\star}\in\mathrm{ri}\big{(}{\partial R(x^{\star})}\big{)}\quad\text{and}\quad-B^{T}\psi^{\star}\in\mathrm{ri}\big{(}{\partial J(y^{\star})}\big{)}.(ND)

Let ℳx⋆R,ℳy⋆Jsubscriptsuperscriptℳ𝑅superscript𝑥⋆subscriptsuperscriptℳ𝐽superscript𝑦⋆\mathcal{M}^{R}_{x^{\star}},\mathcal{M}^{J}_{y^{\star}} be two smooth manifolds around x⋆,y⋆superscript𝑥⋆superscript𝑦⋆x^{\star},y^{\star} respectively, and suppose R∈PSFx⋆​(ℳx⋆R),J∈PSFy⋆​(ℳy⋆J)formulae-sequence𝑅subscriptPSFsuperscript𝑥⋆subscriptsuperscriptℳ𝑅superscript𝑥⋆𝐽subscriptPSFsuperscript𝑦⋆subscriptsuperscriptℳ𝐽superscript𝑦⋆R\in\mathrm{PSF}_{x^{\star}}(\mathcal{M}^{R}_{x^{\star}}),J\in\mathrm{PSF}_{y^{\star}}(\mathcal{M}^{J}_{y^{\star}}) are partly smooth. The above comparisons, particularly for γ=‖K‖210𝛾superscriptnorm𝐾210\gamma=\frac{{|\kern-1.125pt|}K{|\kern-1.125pt|}^{2}}{10} imply that the trajectory of the sequence {zk}k∈ℕsubscriptsubscript𝑧𝑘𝑘ℕ\{{z}_{k}\}_{k\in\mathbb{N}} is crucial for the acceleration outcome of the inertial scheme.Since the trajectories of ADMM depends on the properties of R,J𝑅𝐽R,J and choice of γ𝛾\gamma, this implies that the right scheme that can achieve uniform acceleration despite R,J𝑅𝐽R,J and γ𝛾\gamma should be able to adapt itself to the trajectory of the method.

In Section A, substantial discussions on why inertial ADMM fails, when the trajectory of zksubscript𝑧𝑘{z}_{k} is spiral, are provided. Especially for the case when both functions in (𝒫ADMMsubscript𝒫ADMM\mathcal{P}_{\mathrm{ADMM}}) are (locally) polyhedral around the solution, e.g. the feasibility problem discussed above.

The previous section shows the trajectory of {zk}k∈ℕsubscriptsubscript𝑧𝑘𝑘ℕ\{{z}_{k}\}_{k\in\mathbb{N}} eventually settles onto a regular path i.e. either straight line or spiral. In this section, we exploit this regularity to design adaptive acceleration for ADMM, which is called “A33\!{}^{3}DMM”; See Algorithm 1.

Define the extrapolation parameterized by s,q𝑠𝑞s,q asℰs,q​(zk,⋯,zk−q−1)=defVk​(∑i=1sCki)(:,1),superscriptdefsubscriptℰ𝑠𝑞subscript𝑧𝑘⋯subscript𝑧𝑘𝑞1subscript𝑉𝑘subscriptsuperscriptsubscript𝑖1𝑠superscriptsubscript𝐶𝑘𝑖:1\mathcal{E}_{s,q}({z}_{k},\dotsm,z_{k-q-1})\stackrel{{\scriptstyle\text{\rm\tiny def}}}{{=}}V_{k}{\big{(}}{\mathbin{\scalebox{1.25}{$\sum$}}_{i=1}^{s}C_{k}^{i}}{\big{)}}_{(:,1)},we obtain the following trajectory following adaptive acceleration for ADMM.

In Algorithm 1, we change the order of updates so that the extrapolation step only needs to be carried out on zksubscript𝑧𝑘{z}_{k}. This is due to the fact, the update of yksubscript𝑦𝑘y_{k} only depends on zksubscript𝑧𝑘{z}_{k}, and such an arrangement requires the minimal computational overhead.Under such setting, the extra memory cost is p​q𝑝𝑞pq for the storage of Vksubscript𝑉𝑘V_{k}. The extra computational cost of A3DMM is very small, which is about n​q2𝑛superscript𝑞2nq^{2} for computing the pseudo-inverse of Vksubscript𝑉𝑘V_{k}.Moreover, the value of q𝑞q usually is taken very small, e.g. q≤10𝑞10q\leq 10, therefore the total overheads of A33\!{}^{3}DMM is rather limited.

To discuss the convergence of A33\!{}^{3}DMM, we shall treat the algorithm as a perturbation of the original ADMM.If the perturbation error is absolutely summable, then we obtain the convergence of A33\!{}^{3}DMM.More precisely, let εk∈ℝnsubscript𝜀𝑘superscriptℝ𝑛\varepsilon_{k}\in\mathbb{R}^{n} whose value takesεk={0:mod​(k,q¯)≠0​or​mod​(k,q¯)=0&ρ​(Ck)≥1,ak​ℰs,q​(zk,⋯,zk−q−1):mod​(k,q¯)=0&ρ​(Ck)<1.\varepsilon_{k}=\left\{\begin{aligned} 0&:\mathrm{mod}(k,\bar{q})\neq 0\enskip\textrm{or}\enskip\mathrm{mod}(k,\bar{q})=0\,\,\&\,\,\rho(C_{k})\geq 1,\\a_{k}\mathcal{E}_{s,q}({z}_{k},\dotsm,z_{k-q-1})&:\mathrm{mod}(k,\bar{q})=0\enskip\&\enskip\rho(C_{k})<1.\end{aligned}\right.Suppose the fixed-point formulation of ADMM can be written as zk=ℱ​(zk−1)subscript𝑧𝑘ℱsubscript𝑧𝑘1{z}_{k}=\mathcal{F}({z}_{k-1}) for some ℱℱ\mathcal{F} (see Section C of the appendix for details).Then Algorithm 1 can be written aszk=ℱ​(zk−1+εk−1).subscript𝑧𝑘ℱsubscript𝑧𝑘1subscript𝜀𝑘1{z}_{k}=\mathcal{F}({z}_{k-1}+\varepsilon_{k-1}).(4.4)Owing to (4.4), we can obtain the following convergence for Algorithm 1 which is based on the classic convergence result of inexact Krasnosel’skiĭ-Mann fixed-point iteration [6, Proposition 5.34].

The summability condition ∑k‖εk‖<+∞subscript𝑘normsubscript𝜀𝑘\sum_{k}{|\kern-1.125pt|}\varepsilon_{k}{|\kern-1.125pt|}<+\infty in general cannot be guaranteed. However, it can be enforced by a simple online updating rule. Let a∈[0,1]𝑎01a\in[0,1] and b,δ>0𝑏𝛿0b,\delta>0, then aksubscript𝑎𝑘a_{k} can be determined by ak=min⁡{a,b/(k1+δ​‖zk−zk−1‖)}subscript𝑎𝑘𝑎𝑏superscript𝑘1𝛿normsubscript𝑧𝑘subscript𝑧𝑘1a_{k}=\min\big{\{}a,{b}/(k^{1+\delta}{|\kern-1.125pt|}{z}_{k}-{z}_{k-1}{|\kern-1.125pt|})\big{\}}.

Observe that in A33\!{}^{3}DMM, when A,B𝐴𝐵A,B are non-trivial, in general there are no closed form solutions for xksubscript𝑥𝑘x_{k} and yksubscript𝑦𝑘y_{k}.Take xksubscript𝑥𝑘x_{k} for example, suppose it is computed approximately, then in zksubscript𝑧𝑘{z}_{k} there will be another approximation error εk′superscriptsubscript𝜀𝑘′\varepsilon_{k}^{\prime}, and consequentlyzk=ℱ​(zk−1+εk−1+γ​εk−1′).subscript𝑧𝑘ℱsubscript𝑧𝑘1subscript𝜀𝑘1𝛾superscriptsubscript𝜀𝑘1′{z}_{k}=\mathcal{F}({z}_{k-1}+\varepsilon_{k-1}+\gamma\varepsilon_{k-1}^{\prime}).If there holds ∑k‖εk−1′‖<+∞subscript𝑘normsuperscriptsubscript𝜀𝑘1′\sum_{k}{|\kern-1.125pt|}\varepsilon_{k-1}^{\prime}{|\kern-1.125pt|}<+\infty, Proposition 4.2 remains true for the above perturbation form.

We have so far alluded to the idea that the extrapolated point z¯k,ssubscript¯𝑧𝑘𝑠\bar{z}_{k,s} defined in (4.2) (which depends only on {zk−j}j=0qsuperscriptsubscriptsubscript𝑧𝑘𝑗𝑗0𝑞\{z_{k-j}\}_{j=0}^{q}) is an approximation to zk+ssubscript𝑧𝑘𝑠z_{k+s}. Let {zk}k∈ℕsubscriptsubscript𝑧𝑘𝑘ℕ\{{z}_{k}\}_{k\in\mathbb{N}} be a sequence in ℝnsuperscriptℝ𝑛\mathbb{R}^{n} and let vk=defzk−zk−1superscriptdefsubscript𝑣𝑘subscript𝑧𝑘subscript𝑧𝑘1v_{k}\stackrel{{\scriptstyle\text{\rm\tiny def}}}{{=}}{z}_{k}-{z}_{k-1}. In this section, two variants of ADMM, including the relaxed ADMM and symmetric ADMM which updates ψ𝜓\psi twice every iteration, are discussed.An extension of A3DMM Algorithm 1 to these variants is provided at the end of the section.

In the literature, a popular variant of ADMM is the relaxed ADMM which takes the following iteration procedure:xksubscript𝑥𝑘\displaystyle x_{k}=argminx∈ℝn​R​(x)+γ2​‖A​x+B​yk−1−b+1γ​ψk−1‖2,absentsubscriptargmin𝑥superscriptℝ𝑛𝑅𝑥𝛾2superscriptnorm𝐴𝑥𝐵subscript𝑦𝑘1𝑏1𝛾subscript𝜓𝑘12\displaystyle=\mathrm{argmin}_{x\in\mathbb{R}^{n}}\,R(x)+\tfrac{\gamma}{2}{|\kern-1.125pt|}Ax+By_{k-1}-b+\tfrac{1}{\gamma}\psi_{k-1}{|\kern-1.125pt|}^{2},(5.1)x¯ksubscript¯𝑥𝑘\displaystyle\bar{x}_{k}=ϕ​A​xk−(1−ϕ)​(B​yk−1−b),absentitalic-ϕ𝐴subscript𝑥𝑘1italic-ϕ𝐵subscript𝑦𝑘1𝑏\displaystyle=\phi Ax_{k}-(1-\phi)(By_{k-1}-b),yksubscript𝑦𝑘\displaystyle y_{k}=argminy∈ℝm​J​(y)+γ2​‖x¯k+B​y−b+1γ​ψk−1‖2,absentsubscriptargmin𝑦superscriptℝ𝑚𝐽𝑦𝛾2superscriptnormsubscript¯𝑥𝑘𝐵𝑦𝑏1𝛾subscript𝜓𝑘12\displaystyle=\mathrm{argmin}_{y\in\mathbb{R}^{m}}\,J(y)+\tfrac{\gamma}{2}{|\kern-1.125pt|}\bar{x}_{k}+By-b+\tfrac{1}{\gamma}\psi_{k-1}{|\kern-1.125pt|}^{2},ψksubscript𝜓𝑘\displaystyle\psi_{k}=ψk−1+γ​(x¯k+B​yk−b),absentsubscript𝜓𝑘1𝛾subscript¯𝑥𝑘𝐵subscript𝑦𝑘𝑏\displaystyle=\psi_{k-1}+\gamma(\bar{x}_{k}+By_{k}-b),where ϕ∈[0,2]italic-ϕ02\phi\in[0,2] is the relaxation parameter. In its dual form, the relaxed ADMM is equivalent to the relaxed Douglas–Rachford splitting applied to solve (𝒟ADMMsubscript𝒟ADMM\mathcal{D}_{\mathrm{ADMM}}), see Section C.1.1. As aforementioned, the ADMM iteration (1.1) is equivalent to applying Douglas–Rachford splitting to the dual problem (𝒟ADMMsubscript𝒟ADMM\mathcal{D}_{\mathrm{ADMM}}) [25].It is also pointed out in [25] that, if the Peaceman–Rachford splitting method [42] is applied to solve (𝒟ADMMsubscript𝒟ADMM\mathcal{D}_{\mathrm{ADMM}}), then it leads to the following iteration in the primal formxksubscript𝑥𝑘\displaystyle x_{k}=argminx∈ℝn​R​(x)+γ2​‖A​x+B​yk−1−b+1γ​ψk−1‖2,absentsubscriptargmin𝑥superscriptℝ𝑛𝑅𝑥𝛾2superscriptnorm𝐴𝑥𝐵subscript𝑦𝑘1𝑏1𝛾subscript𝜓𝑘12\displaystyle=\mathrm{argmin}_{x\in\mathbb{R}^{n}}\,R(x)+\tfrac{\gamma}{2}{|\kern-1.125pt|}Ax+By_{k-1}-b+\tfrac{1}{\gamma}\psi_{k-1}{|\kern-1.125pt|}^{2},(5.3)ψk−12subscript𝜓𝑘12\displaystyle\psi_{k-\frac{1}{2}}=ψk−1+γ​(A​xk+B​yk−1−b),absentsubscript𝜓𝑘1𝛾𝐴subscript𝑥𝑘𝐵subscript𝑦𝑘1𝑏\displaystyle=\psi_{k-1}+\gamma(Ax_{k}+By_{k-1}-b),yksubscript𝑦𝑘\displaystyle y_{k}=argminy∈ℝm​J​(y)+γ2​‖A​xk+B​y−b+1γ​ψk−12‖2,absentsubscriptargmin𝑦superscriptℝ𝑚𝐽𝑦𝛾2superscriptnorm𝐴subscript𝑥𝑘𝐵𝑦𝑏1𝛾subscript𝜓𝑘122\displaystyle=\mathrm{argmin}_{y\in\mathbb{R}^{m}}\,J(y)+\tfrac{\gamma}{2}{|\kern-1.125pt|}Ax_{k}+By-b+\tfrac{1}{\gamma}\psi_{k-\frac{1}{2}}{|\kern-1.125pt|}^{2},ψksubscript𝜓𝑘\displaystyle\psi_{k}=ψk−12+γ​(A​xk+B​yk−b),absentsubscript𝜓𝑘12𝛾𝐴subscript𝑥𝑘𝐵subscript𝑦𝑘𝑏\displaystyle=\psi_{k-\frac{1}{2}}+\gamma(Ax_{k}+By_{k}-b),which is also called symmetric ADMM. A brief derivation is provided in Section C.1.2, and we refer to [25, 29] and the references therein for more detailed discussions.

In general, the conditions needed for the convergence of (5.3) is stronger than the standard ADMM (1.1), which is due to the fact that stronger conditions are needed to guarantee the convergence of Peaceman–Rachford splitting method [25]. However, when (5.3) converges, it tends to provide faster performance than (1.1). Similar to (1.2), if we define zk=ψk−γ​B​yk+γ​b=ψk−12+γ​A​xksubscript𝑧𝑘subscript𝜓𝑘𝛾𝐵subscript𝑦𝑘𝛾𝑏subscript𝜓𝑘12𝛾𝐴subscript𝑥𝑘{z}_{k}=\psi_{k}-\gamma By_{k}+\gamma b=\psi_{k-\frac{1}{2}}+\gamma Ax_{k}, then (5.3) is equivalent toxksubscript𝑥𝑘\displaystyle x_{k}=argminx∈ℝn​R​(x)+γ2​‖A​x+1γ​(2​ψk−1−zk−1)‖2,absentsubscriptargmin𝑥superscriptℝ𝑛𝑅𝑥𝛾2superscriptnorm𝐴𝑥1𝛾2subscript𝜓𝑘1subscript𝑧𝑘12\displaystyle=\mathrm{argmin}_{x\in\mathbb{R}^{n}}\,R(x)+\tfrac{\gamma}{2}{|\kern-1.125pt|}Ax+\tfrac{1}{\gamma}(2\psi_{k-1}-{z}_{k-1}){|\kern-1.125pt|}^{2},(5.4)zksubscript𝑧𝑘\displaystyle{z}_{k}=ψk−1+γ​(2​A​xk+B​yk−1−b),absentsubscript𝜓𝑘1𝛾2𝐴subscript𝑥𝑘𝐵subscript𝑦𝑘1𝑏\displaystyle=\psi_{k-1}+\gamma(2Ax_{k}+By_{k-1}-b),yksubscript𝑦𝑘\displaystyle y_{k}=argminy∈ℝm​J​(y)+γ2​‖B​y+1γ​(zk−γ​b)‖2,absentsubscriptargmin𝑦superscriptℝ𝑚𝐽𝑦𝛾2superscriptnorm𝐵𝑦1𝛾subscript𝑧𝑘𝛾𝑏2\displaystyle=\mathrm{argmin}_{y\in\mathbb{R}^{m}}\,J(y)+\tfrac{\gamma}{2}{|\kern-1.125pt|}By+\tfrac{1}{\gamma}({z}_{k}-\gamma b){|\kern-1.125pt|}^{2},ψksubscript𝜓𝑘\displaystyle\psi_{k}=zk+γ​(B​yk−b),absentsubscript𝑧𝑘𝛾𝐵subscript𝑦𝑘𝑏\displaystyle={z}_{k}+\gamma(By_{k}-b),which can be written as the fixed-point iteration in terms of zksubscript𝑧𝑘{z}_{k}, see Section C.1.2.Suppose the iteration is convergent, then following the analysis of Section 2, we can obtain the trajectory property of symmetric ADMM in terms of the fixed-point sequence zksubscript𝑧𝑘{z}_{k}.

From the above discussions, we have that relaxed ADMM (5.2) and symmetric ADMM (5.4) only differ from the standard ADMM on the update of zksubscript𝑧𝑘{z}_{k}. As a result of these similarities, we can easily extend the A3DMM Algorithm 1 to these variants.

Letzk=𝒵​(γ,ϕ;xk,yk−1,ψk−1)subscript𝑧𝑘𝒵𝛾italic-ϕsubscript𝑥𝑘subscript𝑦𝑘1subscript𝜓𝑘1{z}_{k}=\mathcal{Z}(\gamma,\phi;x_{k},y_{k-1},\psi_{k-1})represent the way of updating zksubscript𝑧𝑘{z}_{k} in (1.2), (5.2) and (5.4).By replacing (4.3) in Algorithm 1 with the following equationsyksubscript𝑦𝑘\displaystyle y_{k}=argminy∈ℝm​J​(y)+γ2​‖B​y+1γ​(z¯k−1−γ​b)‖2,absentsubscriptargmin𝑦superscriptℝ𝑚𝐽𝑦𝛾2superscriptnorm𝐵𝑦1𝛾subscript¯𝑧𝑘1𝛾𝑏2\displaystyle=\mathrm{argmin}_{y\in\mathbb{R}^{m}}\,J(y)+{{\frac{\raisebox{0.41669pt}{\footnotesize$\gamma$}}{\raisebox{-1.24994pt}{\footnotesize$2$}}}}{|\kern-1.125pt|}By+\tfrac{1}{\gamma}({\bar{z}}_{k-1}-\gamma b){|\kern-1.125pt|}^{2},(5.5)ψksubscript𝜓𝑘\displaystyle\psi_{k}=z¯k−1+γ​(B​yk−b),absentsubscript¯𝑧𝑘1𝛾𝐵subscript𝑦𝑘𝑏\displaystyle={\bar{z}}_{k-1}+\gamma(By_{k}-b),xksubscript𝑥𝑘\displaystyle x_{k}=argminx∈ℝn​R​(x)+γ2​‖A​x−1γ​(z¯k−1−2​ψk)‖2,absentsubscriptargmin𝑥superscriptℝ𝑛𝑅𝑥𝛾2superscriptnorm𝐴𝑥1𝛾subscript¯𝑧𝑘12subscript𝜓𝑘2\displaystyle=\mathrm{argmin}_{x\in\mathbb{R}^{n}}\,R(x)+{{\frac{\raisebox{0.41669pt}{\footnotesize$\gamma$}}{\raisebox{-1.24994pt}{\footnotesize$2$}}}}{|\kern-1.125pt|}Ax-\tfrac{1}{\gamma}({\bar{z}}_{k-1}-2\psi_{k}){|\kern-1.125pt|}^{2},zksubscript𝑧𝑘\displaystyle{z}_{k}=𝒵​(γ,ϕ;xk,yk−1,ψk−1),absent𝒵𝛾italic-ϕsubscript𝑥𝑘subscript𝑦𝑘1subscript𝜓𝑘1\displaystyle=\mathcal{Z}(\gamma,\phi;x_{k},y_{k-1},\psi_{k-1}),vksubscript𝑣𝑘\displaystyle v_{k}=zk−zk−1andVk=[vk,Vk−1(:,1:q−1)],\displaystyle={z}_{k}-{z}_{k-1}\quad\text{and}\quad V_{k}=[v_{k},V_{k-1}(:,1:q-1)],we obtain the extension of A3DMM to the variants of ADMM.

We present numerical experiments on affine constrained minimisation (e.g. Basis Pursuit), LASSO, quadratic programming and image processing problems to demonstrate the performance of A3DMM.In the numerical comparison below, we mainly compare with the original ADMM and its inertial version (3.1) with fixed ak≡0.3subscript𝑎𝑘0.3a_{k}\equiv 0.3. In the experiment, the cameraman image is used, and 50%percent5050\% of the pixels is removed randomly.The angle θksubscript𝜃𝑘\theta_{k} of ADMM and the comparisons of the four schemes are provided in Figure 7:•Though both functions in (6.5) are polyhedral, since the subproblem of xksubscript𝑥𝑘x_{k} is solved approximately, the eventual angle actually is oscillating instead of being a constant.•Inertial ADMM again is slower than the original ADMM as the trajectory of ADMM is a spiral.•For the two A33\!{}^{3}DMM schemes, their performances are close as previous examples.•For PSNR the image quality assessment, Figure 7(c) implies that A33\!{}^{3}DMM is also the best.We also compare the visual quality of the images obtained by the four schemes for the 303030’th iteration, which is shown below in Figure 8. It can be observed that the image quality (2nd row of Figure 8) is much better than the 1st row of ADMM and inertial ADMM.

In this article, by analyzing the trajectory of the fixed point sequences associated to ADMM and extrapolating along the trajectory, we provide an alternative derivation of these methods. Furthermore, our local linear analysis allows for the application of previous results on extrapolation methods, and hence provides guaranteed (local) acceleration. Extension of the proposed framework to general first-order methods is ongoing.

When η𝜂\eta is real valued, the property of ρ𝜌\rho is well studied, we refer to [37] and references therein for detailed discussions.Basically, we have that|ρ|={(1+a)2​η2≥4​a​η:ρ is real, |ρ|<1 holds for any a∈[0,1],(1+a)2​η2<4​a​η:ρ is complex, |ρ|=a​η<1 holds for any a∈[0,1].{\lvert}\rho{\rvert}=\left\{\begin{aligned} (1+a)^{2}\eta^{2}\geq 4a\eta&:\textrm{$\rho$ is real, ${\lvert}\rho{\rvert}<1$ holds for any $a\in[0,1]$},\\(1+a)^{2}\eta^{2}<4a\eta&:\textrm{$\rho$ is complex, ${\lvert}\rho{\rvert}=\sqrt{a\eta}<1$ holds for any $a\in[0,1]$.}\end{aligned}\right.The above result can be summarized below.

To demonstrate the above result, we consider fixing η𝜂\eta and varying a∈[0,1]𝑎01a\in[0,1]. Two choices of η𝜂\eta are considered η=0.9,0.98𝜂0.90.98\eta=0.9,0.98, the value of |ρ|𝜌{\lvert}\rho{\rvert} is plotted in Figure 9 in black line. It can be observed that |ρ|𝜌{\lvert}\rho{\rvert} is strictly smaller than one for both choices of η𝜂\eta.Note that |ρ|𝜌{\lvert}\rho{\rvert} reaches a minimal value for some a𝑎a, we refer to [37] for detailed discussion on this.

When η𝜂\eta is complex, it can be written as η=|η|​ei​α𝜂𝜂superscriptei𝛼\eta={\lvert}\eta{\rvert}\mathrm{e}^{\mathrm{i}\alpha} where α𝛼\alpha is the argument of η𝜂\eta. Consequently, the FISTA inertial parameter rule still can not be applied, unless the magnitude of the leading eigenvalue is small enough; See Figure 10 (a).

Let T1,T2subscript𝑇1subscript𝑇2T_{1},T_{2} be two subspaces, and without the loss of generality, assume1≤p=defdim(T1)≤q=defdim(T2)≤n−1.1𝑝superscriptdefdimensionsubscript𝑇1𝑞superscriptdefdimensionsubscript𝑇2𝑛11\leq p\stackrel{{\scriptstyle\text{\rm\tiny def}}}{{=}}\dim(T_{1})\leq q\stackrel{{\scriptstyle\text{\rm\tiny def}}}{{=}}\dim(T_{2})\leq n-1.

The following lemma shows the relation between the Friedrichs and principal angles, whose proof can be found in [7, Proposition 3.3].

Let ℳℳ\mathcal{M} be a C2superscript𝐶2C^{2}-smooth embedded submanifold of ℝnsuperscriptℝ𝑛\mathbb{R}^{n} around a point x𝑥x. With some abuse of terminology, we shall state C2superscript𝐶2C^{2}-manifold instead of C2superscript𝐶2C^{2}-smooth embedded submanifold of ℝnsuperscriptℝ𝑛\mathbb{R}^{n}. The natural embedding of a submanifold ℳℳ\mathcal{M} into ℝnsuperscriptℝ𝑛\mathbb{R}^{n} permits to define a Riemannian structure and to introduce geodesics on ℳℳ\mathcal{M}, and we simply say ℳℳ\mathcal{M} is a Riemannian manifold. We denote respectively 𝒯ℳ​(x)subscript𝒯ℳ𝑥\mathcal{T}_{\mathcal{M}}(x) and 𝒩ℳ​(x)subscript𝒩ℳ𝑥\mathcal{N}_{\mathcal{M}}(x) the tangent and normal space of ℳℳ\mathcal{M} at point near x𝑥x in ℳℳ\mathcal{M}.

Geodesics generalize the concept of straight lines in ℝnsuperscriptℝ𝑛\mathbb{R}^{n}, preserving the zero acceleration characteristic, to manifolds. Roughly speaking, a geodesic is locally the shortest path between two points on ℳℳ\mathcal{M}. We denote by 𝔤​(t;x,h)𝔤𝑡𝑥ℎ\mathfrak{g}(t;x,h) the value at t∈ℝ𝑡ℝt\in\mathbb{R} of the geodesic starting at 𝔤​(0;x,h)=x∈ℳ𝔤0𝑥ℎ𝑥ℳ\mathfrak{g}(0;x,h)=x\in\mathcal{M} with velocity 𝔤˙​(t;x,h)=d​𝔤d​t​(t;x,h)=h∈𝒯ℳ​(x)˙𝔤𝑡𝑥ℎ𝑑𝔤𝑑𝑡𝑡𝑥ℎℎsubscript𝒯ℳ𝑥\dot{\mathfrak{g}}(t;x,h)={{\frac{\raisebox{0.26251pt}{\footnotesize$d\mathfrak{g}$}}{\raisebox{-0.78746pt}{\footnotesize$dt$}}}}(t;x,h)=h\in\mathcal{T}_{\mathcal{M}}(x) (which is uniquely defined).For every h∈𝒯ℳ​(x)ℎsubscript𝒯ℳ𝑥h\in\mathcal{T}_{\mathcal{M}}(x), there exists an interval I𝐼I around 00 and a unique geodesic 𝔤​(t;x,h):I→ℳ:𝔤𝑡𝑥ℎ→𝐼ℳ\mathfrak{g}(t;x,h):I\to\mathcal{M} such that 𝔤​(0;x,h)=x𝔤0𝑥ℎ𝑥\mathfrak{g}(0;x,h)=x and 𝔤˙​(0;x,h)=h˙𝔤0𝑥ℎℎ\dot{\mathfrak{g}}(0;x,h)=h.The mappingExpx:𝒯ℳ​(x)→ℳ,h↦Expx​(h)=𝔤​(1;x,h),:subscriptExp𝑥formulae-sequence→subscript𝒯ℳ𝑥ℳmaps-toℎsubscriptExp𝑥ℎ𝔤1𝑥ℎ\mathrm{Exp}_{x}:\mathcal{T}_{\mathcal{M}}(x)\to\mathcal{M},\,\,h\mapsto\mathrm{Exp}_{x}(h)=\mathfrak{g}(1;x,h),is called Exponential map.Given x,x′∈ℳ𝑥superscript𝑥′ℳx,x^{\prime}\in\mathcal{M}, the direction h∈𝒯ℳ​(x)ℎsubscript𝒯ℳ𝑥h\in\mathcal{T}_{\mathcal{M}}(x) we are interested in is such thatExpx​(h)=x′=𝔤​(1;x,h).subscriptExp𝑥ℎsuperscript𝑥′𝔤1𝑥ℎ\mathrm{Exp}_{x}(h)=x^{\prime}=\mathfrak{g}(1;x,h).

Given two points x,x′∈ℳ𝑥superscript𝑥′ℳx,x^{\prime}\in\mathcal{M}, let 𝒯ℳ​(x),𝒯ℳ​(x′)subscript𝒯ℳ𝑥subscript𝒯ℳsuperscript𝑥′\mathcal{T}_{\mathcal{M}}(x),\mathcal{T}_{\mathcal{M}}(x^{\prime}) be their corresponding tangent spaces. Defineτ:𝒯ℳ​(x)→𝒯ℳ​(x′),:𝜏→subscript𝒯ℳ𝑥subscript𝒯ℳsuperscript𝑥′\tau:\mathcal{T}_{\mathcal{M}}(x)\to\mathcal{T}_{\mathcal{M}}(x^{\prime}),the parallel translation along the unique geodesic joining x𝑥x to x′superscript𝑥′x^{\prime}, which is isomorphism and isometry w.r.t. the Riemannian metric.

For a vector v∈𝒩ℳ​(x)𝑣subscript𝒩ℳ𝑥v\in\mathcal{N}_{\mathcal{M}}(x), the Weingarten map of ℳℳ\mathcal{M} at x𝑥x is the operator 𝔚x​(⋅,v):𝒯ℳ​(x)→𝒯ℳ​(x):subscript𝔚𝑥⋅𝑣→subscript𝒯ℳ𝑥subscript𝒯ℳ𝑥\mathfrak{W}_{x}(\cdot,v):\mathcal{T}_{\mathcal{M}}(x)\to\mathcal{T}_{\mathcal{M}}(x) defined by𝔚x​(⋅,v)=−𝒫𝒯ℳ​(x)​d​V​[h],subscript𝔚𝑥⋅𝑣subscript𝒫subscript𝒯ℳ𝑥d𝑉delimited-[]ℎ\mathfrak{W}_{x}(\cdot,v)=-\mathscr{P}_{\mathcal{T}_{\mathcal{M}}(x)}\mathrm{d}V[h],where V𝑉V is any local extension of v𝑣v to a normal vector field on ℳℳ\mathcal{M}. The definition is independent of the choice of the extension V𝑉V, and 𝔚x​(⋅,v)subscript𝔚𝑥⋅𝑣\mathfrak{W}_{x}(\cdot,v) is a symmetric linear operator which is closely tied to the second fundamental form of ℳℳ\mathcal{M}, see [16, Proposition II.2.1].

Let G𝐺G be a real-valued function which is C2superscript𝐶2C^{2} along the ℳℳ\mathcal{M} around x𝑥x. The following lemmas characterize the parallel translation and the Riemannian Hessian of nearby points in ℳℳ\mathcal{M}.

The Riemannian gradient and Hessian of partly smooth functions are covered by the lemma below.

In this part, we present one fundamental result led by partial smoothness, the linearization of proximal mapping.We first discuss the property of the Riemannian Hessian of a partly smooth function. It is well-known that ADMM is equivalent to applying Douglas–Rachford splitting [19] to the dual problem (𝒟ADMMsubscript𝒟ADMM\mathcal{D}_{\mathrm{ADMM}}).Below we first recall the equivalence between ADMM and Douglas–Rachford which was first established in [25], and then use the convergence of Douglas–Rachford splitting which is well established in the literature [6] to conclude the convergence of ADMM.

Proof of Theorem 2.2.
